<p><b><a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail_Ship" title="Royal Mail Ship">RMS</a> <i>Titanic</i></b> was a British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superliner_(passenger_ship)" title="Superliner (passenger ship)">passenger liner</a> that <a href="/wiki/Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Sinking of the RMS Titanic">sank</a> in the North Atlantic Ocean in the early morning of 15 April 1912 after colliding with an <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">iceberg</a> during her <a href="/wiki/Maiden_voyage" title="Maiden voyage">maiden voyage</a> from <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>, UK to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, US. The sinking resulted in the loss of more than 1,500 passengers and crew making it one of the deadliest commercial <a href="/wiki/List_of_maritime_disasters#Peacetime_disasters" title="List of maritime disasters">peacetime maritime disasters</a> in modern history. The RMS <i>Titanic,</i> the <a href="/wiki/List_of_world%27s_longest_ships" title="List of world's longest ships">largest ship afloat</a> at the time it entered service, was the second of three <a href="/wiki/Olympic-class_ocean_liner" title="Olympic-class ocean liner"><i>Olympic</i> class ocean liners</a> operated by the <a href="/wiki/White_Star_Line" title="White Star Line">White Star Line</a>, and was built by the <a href="/wiki/Harland_and_Wolff" title="Harland and Wolff">Harland and Wolff</a> shipyard in <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a> with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Andrews_(shipbuilder)" title="Thomas Andrews (shipbuilder)">Thomas Andrews</a> as her naval architect. Andrews was among those lost in the sinking. On her maiden voyage, she carried 2,224 passengers and crew.</p>
<p>Under the command of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith_(sea_captain)" title="Edward Smith (sea captain)">Edward Smith</a>, the ship's passengers included some of the wealthiest people in the world, as well as hundreds of emigrants from <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Great Britain and Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and elsewhere throughout Europe seeking a new life in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. A wireless telegraph was provided for the convenience of passengers as well as for operational use. Although <i>Titanic</i> had advanced safety features such as watertight compartments and remotely activated watertight doors, there were not enough <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">lifeboats</a> to accommodate all of those aboard due to outdated maritime safety regulations. <i>Titanic</i> only carried enough lifeboats for 1,178 people—slightly more than half of the number on board, and one-third her total capacity.</p>
<p>After leaving Southampton on 10 April 1912, <i>Titanic</i> called at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a> in France and <a href="/wiki/Cobh" title="Cobh">Queenstown</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Cobh" title="Cobh">Cobh</a>) in Ireland before heading west to New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> On 14 April 1912, four days into the crossing and about 375 miles (600 km) south of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, she hit an iceberg at 11:40 p.m. ship's time. The collision caused the ship's hull plates to buckle inwards along her starboard side and opened five of her sixteen watertight compartments to the sea; the ship gradually filled with water. Meanwhile, passengers and some crew members were evacuated in lifeboats, many of which were launched only partly loaded. A disproportionate number of men were left aboard because of a "<a href="/wiki/Women_and_children_first" title="Women and children first">women and children first</a>" protocol followed by some of the officers loading the lifeboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> By 2:20 a.m., she broke apart and foundered, with well over one thousand people still aboard. Just under two hours after <i>Titanic</i> foundered, the Cunard liner <a href="/wiki/RMS_Carpathia" title="RMS Carpathia">RMS <i>Carpathia</i></a> arrived on the scene of the sinking, where she brought aboard an estimated 705 survivors.</p>
<p>The disaster was greeted with worldwide shock and outrage at the huge loss of life and the regulatory and operational failures that had led to it. Public inquiries in Britain and the United States led to <a href="/wiki/Changes_in_safety_practices_after_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Changes in safety practices after the sinking of the RMS Titanic">major improvements in maritime safety</a>. One of their most important legacies was the establishment in 1914 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Safety_of_Life_at_Sea" title="International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea">International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea</a> (SOLAS), which still governs maritime safety today. Additionally, several new wireless regulations were passed around the world in an effort to learn from the many missteps in wireless communications—which could have saved many more passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">wreck of <i>Titanic</i></a> remains on the seabed, split in two and gradually disintegrating at a depth of 12,415 feet (3,784 m). Since her discovery in 1985, thousands of artefacts have been recovered and put on display at museums around the world. <i>Titanic</i> has become one of the most famous ships in history, her memory kept alive by numerous <a href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic_in_popular_culture" title="RMS Titanic in popular culture">books, folk songs, films, exhibits, and memorials</a>.</p>
<p></p>

</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<p>The name <i>Titanic</i> was derived <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">from Greek mythology</a> and meant gigantic. Built in Belfast, Ireland, in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> (as it then was), the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail_Ship" title="Royal Mail Ship">RMS</a> <i>Titanic</i> was the second of the three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_class_ocean_liner" title="Olympic class ocean liner"><i>Olympic</i>-class ocean liners</a>—the first was the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Olympic" title="RMS Olympic">RMS <i>Olympic</i></a> and the third was the <a href="/wiki/HMHS_Britannic" title="HMHS Britannic">HMHS <i>Britannic</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004319_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004319-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> They were by far the largest vessels of the British shipping company <a href="/wiki/White_Star_Line" title="White Star Line">White Star Line</a>'s fleet, which comprised 29 steamers and tenders in 1912.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridgeHall201127_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridgeHall201127-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> The three ships had their genesis in a discussion in mid-1907 between the White Star Line's chairman, <a href="/wiki/J._Bruce_Ismay" title="J. Bruce Ismay">J. Bruce Ismay</a>, and the American financier <a href="/wiki/J._P._Morgan" title="J. P. Morgan">J. P. Morgan</a>, who controlled the White Star Line's parent corporation, the <a href="/wiki/International_Mercantile_Marine_Co." title="International Mercantile Marine Co.">International Mercantile Marine Co.</a> (IMM).</p>
<p>The White Star Line faced a growing challenge from its main rivals <a href="/wiki/Cunard_Line" title="Cunard Line">Cunard</a>, which had just launched the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania"><i>Lusitania</i></a> and the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Mauretania_(1906)" title="RMS Mauretania (1906)"><i>Mauretania</i></a>—the fastest passenger ships then in service—and the German lines <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_America_Line" title="Hamburg America Line">Hamburg America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norddeutscher_Lloyd" title="Norddeutscher Lloyd">Norddeutscher Lloyd</a>. Ismay preferred to compete on size rather than speed and proposed to commission a new class of liners that would be bigger than anything that had gone before as well as being the last word in comfort and luxury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201126_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201126-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The company sought an upgrade in their fleet primarily in response to the Cunard giants but also to replace their oldest pair of passenger ships still in service, being the <a href="/wiki/SS_Teutonic" title="SS Teutonic">SS <i>Teutonic</i></a> of 1889 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SS_Majestic_(1890)" title="SS Majestic (1890)">SS <i>Majestic</i></a> of 1890. <i>Teutonic</i> was replaced by <i>Olympic</i> while <i>Majestic</i> was replaced by <i>Titanic</i>. <i>Majestic</i> would be brought back into her old spot on White Star's New York service after Titanic's loss.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The ships were constructed by the Belfast shipbuilders <a href="/wiki/Harland_and_Wolff" title="Harland and Wolff">Harland and Wolff</a>, who had a long-established relationship with the White Star Line dating back to 1867.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201125_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201125-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Harland and Wolff were given a great deal of latitude in designing ships for the White Star Line; the usual approach was for the latter to sketch out a general concept which the former would take away and turn into a ship design. Cost considerations were relatively low on the agenda and Harland and Wolff was authorised to spend what it needed on the ships, plus a five percent profit margin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201125_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201125-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> In the case of the <i>Olympic</i>-class ships, a cost of £3 million for the first two ships was agreed plus "extras to contract" and the usual five percent fee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201112_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201112-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harland and Wolff put their leading designers to work designing the <i>Olympic</i>-class vessels. The design was overseen by <a href="/wiki/William_Pirrie,_1st_Viscount_Pirrie" title="William Pirrie, 1st Viscount Pirrie">Lord Pirrie</a>, a director of both Harland and Wolff and the White Star Line; <a href="/wiki/Naval_architecture" title="Naval architecture">naval architect</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Andrews_(shipbuilder)" title="Thomas Andrews (shipbuilder)">Thomas Andrews</a>, the managing director of Harland and Wolff's design department; Edward Wilding, Andrews' deputy and responsible for calculating the ship's design, stability and trim; and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Carlisle" title="Alexander Carlisle">Alexander Carlisle</a>, the shipyard's chief draughtsman and general manager.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201114_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201114-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Carlisle's responsibilities included the decorations, equipment and all general arrangements, including the implementation of an efficient <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">lifeboat</a> <a href="/wiki/Davit" title="Davit">davit</a> design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 29 July 1908, Harland and Wolff presented the drawings to J. Bruce Ismay and other White Star Line executives. Ismay approved the design and signed three "letters of agreement" two days later authorising the start of construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199555_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199555-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> At this point the first ship—which was later to become <i>Olympic</i>—had no name, but was referred to simply as "Number 400", as it was Harland and Wolff's four hundredth hull. <i>Titanic</i> was based on a revised version of the same design and was given the number 401.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199556_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199556-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dimensions_and_layout">Dimensions and layout</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cd51-1000g.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="905" data-file-width="2987" height="67" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Cd51-1000g.gif/220px-Cd51-1000g.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Cd51-1000g.gif/330px-Cd51-1000g.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Cd51-1000g.gif/440px-Cd51-1000g.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cd51-1000g.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Titanic</i> in 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was 882 feet 9 inches (269.06 m) long with a maximum breadth of 92 feet 6 inches (28.19 m). Her total height, measured from the base of the keel to the top of the bridge, was 104 feet (32 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199822_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199822-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> She measured 46,328 <a href="/wiki/Gross_register_tonnage" title="Gross register tonnage">gross register tons</a> and with a draught of 34 feet 7 inches (10.54 m), she displaced 52,310 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004efn319_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004efn319-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>All three of the <i>Olympic</i>-class ships had ten decks (excluding the top of the officers' quarters), eight of which were for passenger use. From top to bottom, the decks were:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>The Boat Deck</b>, on which the lifeboats were housed. It was from here during the early hours of 15 April 1912 that <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s lifeboats were lowered into the North Atlantic. The bridge and wheelhouse were at the forward end, in front of the captain's and officers' quarters. The bridge stood 8 feet (2.4 m) above the deck, extending out to either side so that the ship could be controlled while docking. The wheelhouse stood directly behind and above the bridge. The entrance to the First Class <a href="/wiki/Grand_Staircase_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Grand Staircase of the RMS Titanic">Grand Staircase</a> and gymnasium were located midships along with the raised roof of the First Class lounge, while at the rear of the deck were the roof of the First Class smoke room and the relatively modest Second Class entrance. The wood-covered deck was divided into four segregated promenades; for officers, First Class passengers, engineers, and Second Class passengers respectively. Lifeboats lined the side of the deck except in the First Class area, where there was a gap so that the view would not be spoiled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201147_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201147-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010229_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010229-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>A Deck</b>, also called the <b>Promenade Deck</b>, extended along the entire 546 feet (166 m) length of the superstructure. It was reserved exclusively for First Class passengers and contained First Class cabins, the First Class lounge, smoke room, reading and writing rooms and Palm Court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201147_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201147-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>B Deck</b>, the <b>Bridge Deck</b>, was the top weight-bearing deck and the uppermost level of the hull. More First Class passenger accommodation was located here with six palatial staterooms (cabins) featuring their own private promenades. On <i>Titanic</i>, the <i>A La Carte</i> Restaurant and the Café Parisien provided luxury dining facilities to First Class passengers. Both were run by subcontracted chefs and their staff; all were lost in the disaster. The Second Class smoking room and entrance hall were both located on this deck. The raised forecastle of the ship was forward of the Bridge Deck, accommodating Number 1 hatch (the main hatch through to the cargo holds), numerous pieces of machinery and the anchor housings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> Aft of the Bridge Deck was the raised Poop Deck, 106 feet (32 m) long, used as a promenade by Third Class passengers. It was where many of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s passengers and crew made their last stand as the ship sank. The forecastle and Poop Deck were separated from the Bridge Deck by <a href="/wiki/Well_deck" title="Well deck">well decks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010232_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010232-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>C Deck</b>, the <b>Shelter Deck</b>, was the highest deck to run uninterrupted from stem to stern. It included both well decks; the aft one served as part of the Third Class promenade. Crew cabins were housed below the forecastle and Third Class public rooms were housed below the Poop Deck. In between were the majority of First Class cabins and the Second Class library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010233_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010233-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>D Deck</b>, the <b>Saloon Deck</b>, was dominated by three large public rooms—the First Class Reception Room, the First Class Dining Saloon and the Second Class Dining Saloon. An open space was provided for Third Class passengers. First, Second and Third Class passengers had cabins on this deck, with berths for firemen located in the bow. It was the highest level reached by the ship's watertight bulkheads (though only by eight of the fifteen bulkheads).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010235_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010235-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>E Deck</b>, the <b>Upper Deck</b>, was predominantly used for passenger accommodation for all three classes plus berths for cooks, seamen, stewards and trimmers. Along its length ran a long passageway nicknamed <a href="/wiki/Scotland_Road" title="Scotland Road">Scotland Road</a>, in reference to a famous street in Liverpool. Scotland Road was used by Third Class passengers and crew members. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010236_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010236-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>F Deck</b>, the <b>Middle Deck</b>, was the last complete deck and mainly accommodated Second and Third Class passengers and several departments of the crew. The Third Class dining saloon was located here, as were the swimming pool and Turkish bath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010236_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010236-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>G Deck</b>, the <b>Lower Deck</b>, was the lowest complete deck that carried passengers, and had the lowest portholes, just above the waterline. The squash court was located here along with the travelling post office where mail clerks sorted letters and parcels so that they would be ready for delivery when the ship docked. Food was also stored here. The deck was interrupted at several points by <a href="/wiki/Orlop" title="Orlop">orlop</a> (partial) decks over the boiler, engine and turbine rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010237_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010237-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Orlop Decks</b> and the <b>Tank Top</b> were on the lowest level of the ship, below the waterline. The orlop decks were used as cargo spaces, while the Tank Top—the inner bottom of the ship's hull—provided the platform on which the ship's boilers, engines, turbines and electrical generators were housed. This area of the ship was occupied by the engine and boiler rooms, areas which passengers would not be permitted to see. They were connected with higher levels of the ship by flights of stairs; twin spiral stairways near the bow provided access up to D Deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201148-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010237_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010237-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="1000" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg/180px-Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg/270px-Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg/360px-Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_stern_and_rudder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rudder with central and port wing propellers;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup> note the man at bottom of the photo</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was equipped with three main engines—two <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_engine" title="Reciprocating engine">reciprocating</a> four-<a href="/wiki/Cylinder_(engine)" title="Cylinder (engine)">cylinder</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple-expansion" title="Triple-expansion">triple-expansion</a> <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> and one centrally placed low-pressure <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">Parsons turbine</a>—each driving a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propeller_(marine)" title="Propeller (marine)">propeller</a>. The two reciprocating engines had a combined output of 30,000 hp and a further 16,000 hp was contributed by the turbine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199822_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199822-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The White Star Line had used the same combination of engines on an earlier liner, the <a href="/wiki/SS_Laurentic_(1908)" title="SS Laurentic (1908)">SS <i>Laurentic</i></a>, where it had been a great success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010120_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010120-29"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> It provided a good combination of performance and speed; reciprocating engines by themselves were not powerful enough to propel an <i>Olympic</i>-class liner at the desired speeds, while turbines were sufficiently powerful but caused uncomfortable vibrations, a problem that affected the all-turbine Cunard liners <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">Lusitania</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Mauretania_(1906)" title="RMS Mauretania (1906)">Mauretania</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010121_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010121-30"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> By combining reciprocating engines with a turbine, fuel usage could be reduced and motive power increased, while using the same amount of steam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201179_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201179-31"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The two reciprocating engines were each 63 feet (19 m) long and weighed 720 tons, with their bedplates contributing a further 195 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010121_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010121-30"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> They were powered by steam produced in 29 boilers, 24 of which were double-ended and 5 single-ended, which contained a total of 159 furnaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201180_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201180-32"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> The boilers were 15 feet 9 inches (4.80 m) in diameter and 20 feet (6.1 m) long, each weighing 91.5 tons and capable of holding 48.5 tons of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010126_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010126-33"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>They were heated by burning coal, 6,611 tons of which could be carried in <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s bunkers with a further 1,092 tons in Hold 3. The furnaces required over 600 tons of coal a day to be shovelled into them by hand, requiring the services of 176 <a href="/wiki/Fireman_(steam_engine)" title="Fireman (steam engine)">firemen</a> working around the clock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010148_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010148-34"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> 100 tons of ash a day had to be disposed of by ejecting it into the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201186_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201186-35"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The work was relentless, dirty and dangerous, and although firemen were paid relatively generously<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010148_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010148-34"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> there was a high suicide rate among those who worked in that capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201185_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201185-36"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Exhaust steam leaving the reciprocating engines was fed into the turbine, which was situated aft. From there it passed into a condenser, to increase the efficiency of the turbine and so that the steam could be condensed back into water and reused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201196_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201196-37"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The engines were attached directly to long shafts which drove the propellers. There were three, one for each engine; the outer (or wing) propellers were the largest, each carrying three blades of manganese-bronze alloy with a total diameter of 23.5 feet (7.2 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010126_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010126-33"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The middle propeller was slightly smaller at 17 feet (5.2 m) in diameter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010127_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010127-38"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> and could be stopped but not reversed.</p>
<p><i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s electrical plant was capable of producing more power than an average city power station of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201174_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201174-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Immediately aft of the turbine engine were four 400 kW steam-driven electric generators, used to provide electrical power to the ship, plus two 30 kW auxiliary generators for emergency use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011106_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011106-40"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Their location in the stern of the ship meant that they remained operational until the last few minutes before the ship sank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011107_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011107-41"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technique">Technique</span></h3>
<p>The interiors of the <i>Olympic</i>-class ships were subdivided into sixteen primary <a href="/wiki/Compartment_(ship)" title="Compartment (ship)">compartments</a> divided by fifteen bulkheads which extended well above the waterline. Eleven vertically closing watertight doors could seal off the compartments in the event of an emergency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201144_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201144-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The ships' exposed decking was made of pine and teak, while interior ceilings were covered in painted granulated <a href="/wiki/Cork_(material)" title="Cork (material)">cork</a> to combat condensation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010104_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010104-43"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Standing above the decks were four funnels, each painted <a href="/wiki/Buff_(colour)" title="Buff (colour)">buff</a> with black tops, (though only three were functional—the last one was a dummy, installed for aesthetic purposes and also for kitchen ventilation)—and two masts, each 155 feet (47 m) high, which supported derricks for working cargo.</p>
<p><i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s rudder was large enough—at 78 feet 8 inches (23.98 m) high and 15 feet 3 inches (4.65 m) long, weighing over 100 tons—that it required <a href="/wiki/Steering_engine" title="Steering engine">steering engines</a> to move it. Two steam-powered steering engines were installed though only one was used at any one time, with the other one kept in reserve. They were connected to the short <a href="/wiki/Tiller" title="Tiller">tiller</a> through stiff springs, to isolate the steering engines from any shocks in heavy seas or during fast changes of direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201168_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201168-44"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> As a last resort, the tiller could be moved by ropes connected to two steam <a href="/wiki/Capstan_(nautical)" title="Capstan (nautical)">capstans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201170_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201170-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> The capstans were also used to raise and lower the ship's five anchors (one port, one starboard, one in the centreline and two <a href="/wiki/Warping_(sailing)" title="Warping (sailing)">kedging</a> anchors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201170_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201170-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The ship was equipped with her own waterworks, capable of heating and pumping water to all parts of the vessel via a complex network of pipes and valves. The main water supply was taken aboard while <i>Titanic</i> was in port, but in an emergency the ship could also distil fresh water from seawater, though this was not a straightforward process as the distillation plant quickly became clogged by salt deposits. A network of insulated ducts conveyed warm air, driven by electric fans, around the ship, and First Class cabins were fitted with additional electric heaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201174_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201174-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was equipped with two 1.5 kW <a href="/wiki/Spark-gap_transmitter" title="Spark-gap transmitter">quenched spark-gap transmitters</a> for <a href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraphy" title="Wireless telegraphy">wireless telegraphy</a> located in the radio room on the Boat Deck, in the Officers' quarters. One set was used for transmitting messages and the other, located in a soundproofed booth called the "Silent Room", for receiving them. The signals were transmitted through two parallel wires strung between the ship's masts, 50 feet (15 m) above the funnels to avoid the corrosive smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201174_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201174-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The system was one of the most powerful in the world, with a range of up to 1,000 miles (1,609 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010165_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010165-46"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> It was owned and operated by the <a href="/wiki/Marconi_Company" title="Marconi Company">Marconi International Marine Communication Company</a> rather than the White Star Line, and was intended primarily for passengers rather than ship operations. The function of the two wireless operators—both Marconi employees—was to operate a 24-hour service sending and receiving wireless telegrams for passengers. They did, however, also pass on professional ship messages such as weather reports and ice warnings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010162_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010162-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passenger_facilities">Passenger facilities</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/First_class_facilities_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="First class facilities of the RMS Titanic">First class facilities of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<p>The passenger facilities aboard <i>Titanic</i> aimed to meet the highest standards of luxury. According to <i>Titanic'</i>s general arrangement plans, the ship could accommodate 833 First Class Passengers, 614 in Second Class and 1,006 in Third Class, for a total passenger capacity of 2,453. In addition, her capacity for crew members exceeded 900, as most documents of her original configuration have stated that her full carrying capacity for both passengers and crew was approximately 3,547. Her interior design was a departure from that of other passenger liners, which had typically been decorated in the rather heavy style of a <a href="/wiki/Manor_house" title="Manor house">manor house</a> or an <a href="/wiki/English_country_house" title="English country house">English country house</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201157_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201157-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was laid out in a much lighter style similar to that of contemporary high-class hotels—the <a href="/wiki/The_Ritz_London_Hotel" title="The Ritz London Hotel">Ritz Hotel</a> was a reference point—with First Class cabins finished in the <a href="/wiki/Empire_style" title="Empire style">Empire style</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201157_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201157-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> A variety of other decorative styles, ranging from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> to <a href="/wiki/Victorian_decorative_arts" title="Victorian decorative arts">Victorian</a>, were used to decorate cabins and public rooms in First and Second Class areas of the ship. The aim was to convey an impression that the passengers were in a floating hotel rather than a ship; as one passenger recalled, on entering the ship's interior a passenger would "at once lose the feeling that we are on board ship, and seem instead to be entering the hall of some great house on shore".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010182_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010182-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Passengers could use an on-board telephone system, a lending library and a large barber shop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWels199734_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWels199734-50"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The First Class section had a swimming pool, a gymnasium, a <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a> court, a <a href="/wiki/Turkish_bath" title="Turkish bath">Turkish bath</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Electric_bath" title="Electric bath">electric bath</a> and a Verandah Cafe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010182_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010182-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> First Class common rooms were adorned with ornate wood panelling, expensive furniture and other decorations, while the Third Class general room had pine panelling and sturdy teak furniture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMNI_3rd_Class_General_Room_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMNI_3rd_Class_General_Room-51"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i>Café Parisien</i> was located on a sunlit veranda fitted with trellis decorations and offered the best French <i>haute cuisine</i> for First Class passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMNI_1st_Class_Cafe_Parisien_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMNI_1st_Class_Cafe_Parisien-52"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Third Class (also commonly referred to as Steerage) accommodations aboard <i>Titanic</i> were not as luxurious as First Class, but even so were better than on many other ships of the time. They reflected the improved standards which the White Star Line had adopted for trans-Atlantic immigrant and lower-class travel. On most other North Atlantic passenger ships at the time, Third Class accommodations consisted of little more than open dormitories in the forward end of the vessels, in which hundreds of people were confined, often without adequate food or toilet facilities.</p>
<p>The White Star Line had long since broken that mould. As seen aboard <i>Titanic</i>, all White Star Line passenger ships divided their Third Class accommodations into two sections, always at opposite ends of the vessel from one another. The established arrangement was that single men were quartered in the forward areas, while single women, married couples and families were quartered aft. In addition, while other ships provided only open berth sleeping arrangements, White Star Line vessels provided their Third Class passengers with private, small but comfortable cabins capable of accommodating two, four, six, eight and ten passengers.</p>
<p>Third Class accommodations also included their own dining rooms, as well as public gathering areas including adequate open deck space, which aboard <i>Titanic</i> included the Forecastle Deck forward, the Poop Deck aft, both well decks and a large open space on D Deck which could be used as a social hall. This was supplemented by the addition of a smoking room for men and a reading room for women, and although they were not as glamorous in design as spaces seen in upper class accommodations, they were still far above average for the period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Leisure facilities were provided for all three classes to pass the time. As well as making use of the indoor amenities such as the library, smoking rooms, and gymnasium, it was also customary for passengers to socialise on the open deck, promenading or relaxing in hired deck chairs or wooden benches. A passenger list was published before the sailing to inform the public which members of the great and good were on board, and it was not uncommon for ambitious mothers to use the list to identify rich bachelors to whom they could introduce their marriageable daughters during the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010189_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010189-53"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s most distinctive features was her First Class staircase, known as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Staircase_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Grand Staircase of the RMS Titanic">Grand Staircase</a> or Grand Stairway. This descended through seven decks of the ship, from the Boat Deck to E deck in the elegant style depicted in photographs and movies, and then as a more functional and less elegant staircase from there down to F deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201159_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201159-54"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> It was capped with a dome of wrought iron and glass that admitted natural light. Each landing off the staircase gave access to ornate entrance halls lit by gold-plated light fixtures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch199253_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch199253-55"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the uppermost landing was a large carved wooden panel containing a clock, with figures of "Honour and Glory Crowning Time" flanking the clock face.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201159_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201159-54"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The Grand Staircase was destroyed in <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s sinking and is now just a void in the ship which modern explorers have used to access the lower decks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1992207_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1992207-56"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> During the filming of James Cameron's <i>Titanic</i> in 1997, his replica of the Grand Staircase was ripped from its foundations by the force of the inrushing water on the set. It has been suggested that during the real event, the entire Grand Staircase was ejected upwards through the dome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerideth2003236_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerideth2003236-57"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p><br style="clear:both;"/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mail_and_cargo">Mail and cargo</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Circassienne_au_Bain,_After_Blondel.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2145" data-file-width="1497" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/La_Circassienne_au_Bain%2C_After_Blondel.JPG/220px-La_Circassienne_au_Bain%2C_After_Blondel.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/La_Circassienne_au_Bain%2C_After_Blondel.JPG/330px-La_Circassienne_au_Bain%2C_After_Blondel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/La_Circassienne_au_Bain%2C_After_Blondel.JPG/440px-La_Circassienne_au_Bain%2C_After_Blondel.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Circassienne_au_Bain,_After_Blondel.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Copy After <a href="/wiki/Merry-Joseph_Blondel" title="Merry-Joseph Blondel">Merry-Joseph Blondel</a> of the neoclassical oil painting <i><a href="/wiki/La_Circassienne_au_Bain" title="La Circassienne au Bain">La Circassienne au Bain</a></i>; the most highly valued single item of cargo lost on the Titanic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_1913.2C_p.28_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_1913.2C_p.28-58"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although <i>Titanic</i> was primarily a passenger liner, she also carried a substantial amount of cargo. Her designation as a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail_Ship" title="Royal Mail Ship">Royal Mail Ship</a> (RMS) indicated that she carried mail under contract with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> (and also for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Post_Office_Department" title="United States Post Office Department">United States Post Office Department</a>). For the storage of letters, parcels and specie (bullion, coins and other valuables) 26,800 cubic feet (760 m<sup>3</sup>) of space in her holds was allocated. The Sea Post Office on G Deck was manned by five postal clerks, three Americans and two Britons, who worked thirteen hours a day, seven days a week sorting up to 60,000 items daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010146_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010146-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The ship's passengers brought with them a huge amount of baggage; another 19,455 cubic feet (550.9 m<sup>3</sup>) was taken up by first- and second-class baggage. In addition, there was a considerable quantity of regular cargo, ranging from furniture to foodstuffs and even motor cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010146_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010146-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite later myths, the cargo on <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s maiden voyage was fairly mundane; there was no gold, <a href="/wiki/Raise_the_Titanic!" title="Raise the Titanic!">exotic minerals</a> or diamonds, and one of the more famous items lost in the shipwreck, a jewelled copy of the <i><a href="/wiki/Rubaiyat_of_Omar_Khayyam" title="Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam">Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam</a></i>, was valued at only £405 (£34,986 today).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987131_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987131-60"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the claims for compensation filed with Commissioner Gilchrist, following the conclusion of the Senate Inquiry, the single most highly valued item of luggage or cargo was a large neoclassical oil painting entitled <i><a href="/wiki/La_Circassienne_au_Bain" title="La Circassienne au Bain">La Circassienne au Bain</a></i> by French artist <a href="/wiki/Merry-Joseph_Blondel" title="Merry-Joseph Blondel">Merry-Joseph Blondel</a>. The painting’s owner, first class passenger <a href="/wiki/Mauritz_H%C3%A5kan_Bj%C3%B6rnstr%C3%B6m-Steffansson" title="Mauritz Håkan Björnström-Steffansson">Mauritz Håkan Björnström-Steffansson</a>, filed a claim for $100,000 ($2.4 million equivalent in 2014) in compensation for the loss of the artwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_1913.2C_p.28_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_1913.2C_p.28-58"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was equipped with eight electric cranes, four electric winches and three steam winches to lift cargo and baggage in and out of the hold. It is estimated that the ship used some 415 tons of coal whilst in Southampton, simply generating steam to operate the cargo winches and provide heat and light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifeboats">Lifeboats</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lifeboats_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Lifeboats of the RMS Titanic">Lifeboats of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_lifeboat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1880" data-file-width="2759" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Titanic_lifeboat.jpg/250px-Titanic_lifeboat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Titanic_lifeboat.jpg/375px-Titanic_lifeboat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Titanic_lifeboat.jpg/500px-Titanic_lifeboat.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_lifeboat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A collapsible lifeboat with canvas sides</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> carried a total of 20 lifeboats: 14 standard wooden Harland and Wolff lifeboats with a capacity of 65 people each and four Englehardt "collapsible" (wooden bottom, collapsible canvas sides) lifeboats (identified as A to D) with a capacity of 47 people each. In addition, she had two emergency <a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)#Rowing" title="Cutter (boat)">cutters</a> with a capacity of 40 people each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011112_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011112-62"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Olympic</i> herself did not even carry the four collapsibles A–D during the 1911–12 season. All of the lifeboats were stowed securely on the boat deck and, except for collapsible lifeboats A and B, connected to <a href="/wiki/Davit" title="Davit">davits</a> by ropes. Those on the starboard side were odd-numbered 1–15 from bow to stern, while those on the port side were even-numbered 2–16 from bow to stern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord199778_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord199778-64"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The two cutters were kept swung out, hanging from the davits, ready for immediate use, while collapsible lifeboats C and D were stowed on the boat deck (connected to davits) immediately inboard of boats 1 and 2 respectively. A and B were stored on the roof of the officers' quarters, on either side of number 1 funnel. There were no davits to lower them and their weight would make them difficult to launch by hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord199778_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord199778-64"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Each boat carried (among other things) food, water, blankets, and a spare life belt. Lifeline ropes on the boats' sides enabled them to save additional people from the water if necessary.</p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> had 16 sets of davits, each able to handle 4 lifeboats. This gave <i>Titanic</i> the ability to carry up to 64 wooden lifeboats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside200426_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside200426-65"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> which would have been enough for 4,000 people—considerably more than her actual capacity. However, the White Star Line decided that only 16 wooden lifeboats and four collapsibles would be carried, which could accommodate 1,178 people, only one-third of <i>Titanic</i> '​s total capacity. At the time, the Board of Trade's regulations required British vessels over 10,000 tons to only carry 16 lifeboats with a capacity of 990 occupants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011112_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011112-62"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Therefore, the White Star Line actually provided more lifeboat accommodation than was legally required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199838_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199838-66"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup> At the time, lifeboats were intended to ferry survivors from a sinking ship to a rescuing ship—not keep afloat the whole population or power them to shore. Had the <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Californian" title="SS Californian">SS Californian</a></i> responded to <i>Titanic'</i>s distress calls, the lifeboats may have been adequate to ferry the passengers to safety as planned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj1-69"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_and_preparing_the_ship">Building and preparing the ship</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction.2C_launch_and_fitting-out">Construction, launch and fitting-out</span></h3>
<p>The sheer size of <i>Titanic</i> and her sister ships posed a major engineering challenge for Harland and Wolff; no shipbuilder had ever before attempted to construct vessels this large. The ships were constructed on Queen's Island, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Titanic_Quarter,_Belfast" title="Titanic Quarter, Belfast">Titanic Quarter</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_Belfast" title="Port of Belfast">Belfast Harbour</a>. Harland and Wolff had to demolish three existing <a href="/wiki/Slipway" title="Slipway">slipways</a> and build two new ones, the biggest ever constructed up to that time, to accommodate the giant ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201112_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201112-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Their construction was facilitated by an enormous gantry built by <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Arrol_%26_Co." title="Sir William Arrol &amp; Co.">Sir William Arrol &amp; Co.</a>, a Scottish firm responsible for the building of the <a href="/wiki/Forth_Bridge" title="Forth Bridge">Forth Bridge</a> and London's <a href="/wiki/Tower_Bridge" title="Tower Bridge">Tower Bridge</a>. The Arrol Gantry stood 228 feet (69 m) high, was 270 feet (82 m) wide and 840 feet (260 m) long, and weighed more than 6,000 tons. It accommodated a number of mobile cranes. A separate floating crane, capable of lifting 200 tons, was brought in from Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill201078_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill201078-70"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The construction of <i>Titanic</i> and <i>Olympic</i> took place virtually in parallel, with <i>Olympic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s hull laid down first on 16 December 1908 and <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s on 31 March 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199556_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199556-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Both ships took about 26 months to build and followed much the same construction process. They were designed essentially as an enormous floating <a href="/wiki/Box_girder" title="Box girder">box girder</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> acting as a backbone and the frames of the hull forming the ribs. At the base of the ships, a double bottom 5 feet 3 inches (1.60 m) deep supported 300 frames, each between 24 inches (61 cm) and 36 inches (91 cm) apart and measuring up to about 66 feet (20 m) long. They terminated at the bridge deck (B Deck) and were covered with steel plates which formed the outer skin of the ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201142_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201142-71"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 2,000 hull plates were single pieces of rolled steel, mostly up to 6 feet (1.8 m) wide and 30 feet (9.1 m) long and weighing between 2.5 and 3 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201143_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201143-72"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Their thickness varied from 1 inch (2.5 cm) to 1.5 inches (3.8 cm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201144_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201144-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The plates were laid in a <a href="/wiki/Clinker_(boat_building)" title="Clinker (boat building)">clinkered</a> (overlapping) fashion from the keel to the bilge. Above that point they were laid in the "in and out" fashion, where <a href="/wiki/Strake" title="Strake">strake</a> plating was applied in bands (the "in strakes") with the gaps covered by the "out strakes", overlapping on the edges. <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">Steel welding</a> was still in its infancy so the structure had to be held together with over three million iron and steel <a href="/wiki/Rivet" title="Rivet">rivets</a> which by themselves weighed over 1,200 tons. They were fitted using hydraulic machines or were hammered in by hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill201087_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill201087-73"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 1990s some material scientists concluded<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFelkinsLeighlyJankovic1998_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFelkinsLeighlyJankovic1998-74"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> that the steel used for the ship was subject to being especially brittle when cold, and that this brittleness exacerbated the impact damage and hastened the sinking. It is believed that, by the standards of the time, the steel's <a href="/wiki/Quality_(business)" title="Quality (business)">quality</a> was good, not faulty, but that it was inferior to what would be used for shipbuilding purposes in later decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFelkinsLeighlyJankovic1998_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFelkinsLeighlyJankovic1998-74"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The work of constructing the ships was difficult and dangerous. For the 15,000 men who worked at Harland and Wolff at the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010105_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010105-75"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> safety precautions were rudimentary at best; a lot of the work was dangerous and was carried out without any safety equipment like hard hats or hand guards on machinery. As a result, deaths and injuries were to be expected. During <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s construction, 246 injuries were recorded, 28 of them "severe", such as arms severed by machines or legs crushed under falling pieces of steel. Six people died on the ship herself while she was being constructed and fitted out and another two died in the shipyard workshops and sheds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010109_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010109-76"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Just before the launch a worker was killed when a piece of wood fell on him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201133_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201133-77"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was launched at 12:15 p.m. on 31 May 1911 in the presence of Lord Pirrie, J. Pierpoint Morgan and J. Bruce Ismay and 100,000 onlookers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201115_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201115-78"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> 22 tons of soap and tallow were spread on the slipway to lubricate the ship's passage into the <a href="/wiki/River_Lagan" title="River Lagan">River Lagan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201133_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201133-77"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> In keeping with the White Star Line's traditional policy, the ship was not formally named or christened with champagne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201115_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201115-78"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The ship was towed to a fitting-out berth where, over the course of the next year, her engines, funnels and superstructure were installed and her interior was fitted out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201118_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201118-79"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although <i>Titanic</i> was virtually identical to the class's lead ship <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Olympic" title="RMS Olympic">Olympic</a></i>, a few changes were made to differentiate the two ships. The most noticeable of these was that <i>Titanic</i> (and the third vessel in class <i><a href="/wiki/HMHS_Britannic" title="HMHS Britannic">Britannic</a></i>) had a steel screen with sliding windows installed along the forward half of the A Deck promenade. This was installed as a last minute change at the personal request of Bruce Ismay, and was intended to provide additional shelter to first class passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marriot_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marriot-80"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> These changes made <i>Titanic</i> marginally heavier than her sister, and thus she could claim to be the largest ship afloat. The work took longer than expected due to design changes ordered by Ismay and a temporary pause in work occasioned by the need to repair <i>Olympic</i>, which had been in a collision in September 1911. Had <i>Titanic</i> been finished earlier, she might well have missed her collision with an iceberg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201133_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201133-77"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p><br style="clear:both;"/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_trials">Sea trials</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1416" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/RMS_Titanic_2.jpg/250px-RMS_Titanic_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/RMS_Titanic_2.jpg/375px-RMS_Titanic_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/RMS_Titanic_2.jpg/500px-RMS_Titanic_2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Titanic</i> leaving Belfast for her sea trials on 2 April 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s sea trials began at 6 a.m. on Tuesday, 2 April 1912, just two days after her fitting out was finished and eight days before she was due to leave Southampton on her maiden voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi199822_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi199822-81"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> The trials were delayed for a day due to bad weather, but by Monday morning it was clear and fair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199544_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199544-82"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Aboard were 78 stokers, greasers and firemen, and 41 members of crew. No domestic staff appear to have been aboard. Representatives of various companies travelled on <i>Titanic</i> '​s sea trials, Thomas Andrews and Edward Wilding of Harland and Wolff and Harold A. Sanderson of IMM. Bruce Ismay and Lord Pirrie were too ill to attend. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Phillips_(wireless_officer)" title="Jack Phillips (wireless officer)">Jack Phillips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bride" title="Harold Bride">Harold Bride</a> served as radio operators, and performed fine-tuning of the Marconi equipment. Francis Carruthers, a surveyor from the Board of Trade, was also present to see that everything worked, and that the ship was fit to carry passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199544.2C_46_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199544.2C_46-83"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The sea trials consisted of a number of tests of her handling characteristics, carried out first in <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Lough" title="Belfast Lough">Belfast Lough</a> and then in the open waters of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>. Over the course of about twelve hours, <i>Titanic</i> was driven at different speeds, her turning ability was tested and a "crash stop" was performed in which the engines were reversed full ahead to full astern, bringing her to a stop in 850 yd (777 m) or 3 minutes and 15 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside200439.E2.80.9340_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside200439.E2.80.9340-84"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> The ship covered a distance of about 80 nautical miles (92 mi; 150 km), averaging 18 knots (21 mph; 33 km/h) and reaching a maximum speed of just under 21 knots (24 mph; 39 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199545_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199545-85"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On returning to Belfast at about 7 p.m., the surveyor signed an "Agreement and Account of Voyages and Crew", valid for twelve months, which declared the ship seaworthy. An hour later, <i>Titanic</i> left Belfast again—as it turned out, for the last time—to head to Southampton, a voyage of about 570 nautical miles (660 mi; 1,060 km). After a journey lasting about 28 hours she arrived about midnight on 4 April and was towed to the port's Berth 44, ready for the arrival of her passengers and the remainder of her crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199546_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199546-86"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Maiden_voyage">Maiden voyage</span></h2>
<p>Both <i>Olympic</i> and <i>Titanic</i> registered <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> as their home port. The offices of the White Star Line as well as Cunard were in Liverpool, and up until the introduction of the <i>Olympic</i>, most British ocean liners for both Cunard and White Star, such as <i>Lusitania</i> and <i>Mauretania</i>, sailed out of Liverpool followed by a port of call in Ireland. However, the <i>Olympic</i> class liners were to sail out of the port of Southampton on England's southern coast. Southampton had many advantages over Liverpool, the first being its closer proximity to London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199821_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199821-87"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, Southampton, being on England's southern coast, allowed ships to easily cross the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> and make a port of call in northern France, usually at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a>. This allowed British ships to pick up clientele from continental Europe before recrossing the channel and picking up passengers in southern Ireland. The <i>Southampton-Cherbourg-New York run</i> would become so popular that most British ocean liners began using the port after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. Out of respect for Liverpool, ships continued to be registered there until the early 1960s. <i><a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_2" title="Queen Elizabeth 2">Queen Elizabeth 2</a></i> was one of the first ships registered in Southampton when introduced into service by Cunard in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199821_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199821-87"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s maiden voyage was intended to be the first of many cross-Atlantic journeys between Southampton in England, Cherbourg in France, Queenstown in Ireland and New York in the United States, returning via <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a> in England on the eastbound leg. Indeed, her entire schedule of voyages through to December 1912 still exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> The White Star Line intended to operate three ships on that route: <i>Titanic</i>, <i>Olympic</i> and the smaller <a href="/wiki/RMS_Oceanic_(1899)" title="RMS Oceanic (1899)">RMS <i>Oceanic</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leaflet_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leaflet-89"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Each would sail once every three weeks from Southampton and New York, usually leaving at noon each Wednesday from Southampton and each Saturday from New York, thus enabling the White Star Line to offer weekly sailings in each direction. Special trains were scheduled from London and Paris to convey passengers to Southampton and Cherbourg respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leaflet_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leaflet-89"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> The deep-water dock at Southampton, then known as the "<i>White Star Dock</i><span style="padding-left:.2em;">"</span>, had been specially constructed to accommodate the new Olympic-class liners, and had opened in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table align="left" style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">
<tr>
<td>
<table style="width:260px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">
<tr>
<td style="height:240px;border:1px solid #CCCCCC;background-color:#F8F8F8;padding:0px;text-align:center">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EJ_Smith.jpg" title="Edward Smith, captain of Titanic, in 1911"><img alt="Photograph of a bearded man wearing a white captain's uniform, standing on a ship with his arms crossed" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="545" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/EJ_Smith.jpg/171px-EJ_Smith.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/EJ_Smith.jpg/257px-EJ_Smith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/EJ_Smith.jpg/342px-EJ_Smith.jpg 2x" width="171"/></a></div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
