<p><br style="clear:both;"/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew">Crew</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crew_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Crew of the RMS Titanic">Crew of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> had around 885 crew members on board for her maiden voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.93111_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.93111-91"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Like other vessels of her time, she did not have a permanent crew, and the vast majority of crew members were casual workers who only came aboard the ship a few hours before she sailed from Southampton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt200984_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt200984-92"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> The process of signing up recruits had begun on 23 March and some had been sent to Belfast, where they served as a skeleton crew during <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s sea trials and passage to England at the start of April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt200983_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt200983-93"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Captain <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith_(sea_captain)" title="Edward Smith (sea captain)">Edward John Smith</a>, the most senior of the White Star Line's captains, was transferred from <i>Olympic</i> to take command of <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201143.E2.80.9344_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201143.E2.80.9344-94"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Tingle_Wilde" title="Henry Tingle Wilde">Henry Tingle Wilde</a> also came across from <i>Olympic</i> to take the post of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_Mate" title="Chief Mate">Chief Mate</a>. <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s previously designated Chief Mate and First Officer, <a href="/wiki/William_McMaster_Murdoch" title="William McMaster Murdoch">William McMaster Murdoch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lightoller" title="Charles Lightoller">Charles Lightoller</a>, were bumped down to the ranks of First and Second Officer respectively. The original Second Officer, <a href="/wiki/David_Blair_(mariner)" title="David Blair (mariner)">David Blair</a>, was dropped altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010241_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010241-95"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s crew were divided into three principal departments: Deck, with 66 crew; Engine, with 325; and Victualling, with 494.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt200992_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt200992-97"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> The vast majority of the crew were thus not seamen, but were either engineers, firemen, or stokers, responsible for looking after the engines, or stewards and galley staff, responsible for the passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998238_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998238-98"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Of these, over 97% were male; just 23 of the crew were female, mainly stewardesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010242_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010242-99"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The rest represented a great variety of professions—bakers, chefs, butchers, fishmongers, dishwashers, stewards, gymnasium instructors, laundrymen, waiters, bed-makers, cleaners, and even a printer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010242_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010242-99"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> who produced a daily newspaper for passengers called the <i>Atlantic Daily Bulletin</i> with the latest news received by the ship's wireless operators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010162_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010162-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>g<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Most of the crew signed on in Southampton on 6 April;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199556_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199556-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> in all, 699 of the crew came from there, and 40 percent were natives of the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010242_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010242-99"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> A few specialist staff were self-employed or were subcontractors. These included the five postal clerks, who worked for the Royal Mail and the United States Post Office Department, the staff of the First Class <i>A La Carte</i> Restaurant and the Café Parisien, the radio operators (who were employed by Marconi) and the eight musicians, who were employed by an agency and travelled as second-class passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt200950_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt200950-102"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Crew pay varied greatly, from Captain Smith's £105 a month (equivalent to £9,071 today) to the £3 10<i><a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_coin)" title="Shilling (British coin)">s</a></i> (£302 today) that stewardesses earned. The lower-paid victualling staff could, however, supplement their wages substantially through tips from passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010246_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010246-100"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passengers">Passengers</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Passengers_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Passengers of the RMS Titanic">Passengers of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="408" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg/170px-John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg/255px-John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg/340px-John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Jacob_Astor_1909.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_IV" title="John Jacob Astor IV">John Jacob Astor IV</a> in 1909. He was the wealthiest person aboard <i>Titanic</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s passengers numbered around 1,317 people: 324 in First Class, 284 in Second Class. and 709 in Third Class. Of these, 869 (66%) were male and 447 (34%) female. There were 107 children aboard, the largest number of which were in Third Class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt200993_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt200993-103"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> The ship was considerably under capacity on her maiden voyage, as she could accommodate 2,566 passengers—1,034 First Class, 510 Second Class, and 1,022 Third Class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells199918_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells199918-104"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Usually, a high prestige vessel like <i>Titanic</i> could expect to be fully booked on its maiden voyage. However, a <a href="/wiki/National_coal_strike_of_1912" title="National coal strike of 1912">national coal strike</a> in the UK had caused considerable disruption to shipping schedules in the spring of 1912, causing many crossings to be cancelled. Many would-be passengers chose to postpone their travel plans until the strike was over. The strike had finished a few days before <i>Titanic</i> sailed; however, that was too late to have much of an effect. <i>Titanic</i> was able to sail on the scheduled date only because coal was transferred from other vessels which were tied up at Southampton, such as <i><a href="/wiki/SS_City_of_New_York" title="SS City of New York">City of New York</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Oceanic_(1899)" title="RMS Oceanic (1899)">Oceanic</a></i> as well as coal <i>Olympic</i> had brought back from a previous voyage to New York and which had been stored at the White Star Dock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marriot_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marriot-80"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the most prominent people of the day booked a passage aboard <i>Titanic</i>, travelling in First Class. Among them were the American millionaire <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_IV" title="John Jacob Astor IV">John Jacob Astor IV</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Astor" title="Madeleine Astor">Madeleine Force Astor</a>, industrialist <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Guggenheim" title="Benjamin Guggenheim">Benjamin Guggenheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macy%27s" title="Macy's">Macy's</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Isidor_Straus" title="Isidor Straus">Isidor Straus</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Ida_Straus" title="Ida Straus">Ida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a> millionairess <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Brown" title="Margaret Brown">Margaret "Molly" Brown</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>h<span>]</span></a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Sir_Cosmo_Duff-Gordon,_5th_Baronet" title="Sir Cosmo Duff-Gordon, 5th Baronet">Cosmo Duff Gordon</a> and his wife, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Couturi%C3%A8re" title="Couturière">couturière</a> <a href="/wiki/Lucy,_Lady_Duff-Gordon" title="Lucy, Lady Duff-Gordon">Lucy (Lady Duff-Gordon)</a>, cricketer and businessman <a href="/wiki/John_Thayer_(cricketer)" title="John Thayer (cricketer)">John Borland Thayer</a> with his wife <a href="/wiki/Marian_Thayer" title="Marian Thayer">Marian</a> together with their son <a href="/wiki/Jack_Thayer" title="Jack Thayer">Jack</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countess_of_Rothes" title="Countess of Rothes">Countess of Rothes</a>, author and socialite <a href="/wiki/Helen_Churchill_Candee" title="Helen Churchill Candee">Helen Churchill Candee</a>, journalist and social reformer <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_Stead" title="William Thomas Stead">William Thomas Stead</a>, author <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Futrelle" title="Jacques Futrelle">Jacques Futrelle</a> with his wife May, and silent film actress <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Gibson" title="Dorothy Gibson">Dorothy Gibson</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Passengers_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Passengers-106"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s owner J. P. Morgan was scheduled to travel on the maiden voyage, but cancelled at the last minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010Chapter_8_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010Chapter_8-107"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Also aboard the ship were the White Star Line's managing director J. Bruce Ismay and <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s designer Thomas Andrews, who was on board to observe any problems and assess the general performance of the new ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199818_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199818-108"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The exact number of people aboard is not known as not all of those who had booked tickets made it to the ship; about fifty people cancelled for various reasons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199573_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199573-109"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> and not all of those who boarded stayed aboard for the entire journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> Fares varied depending on class and season. Third Class fares from London, Southampton or Queenstown cost £7 5<i>s</i> (equivalent to £626 today) while the cheapest First Class fares cost £23 (£1,987 today).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leaflet_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leaflet-89"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> The most expensive First Class suites were to have cost up to £870 in high season (£75,156 today).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells199918_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells199918-104"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collecting_passengers">Collecting passengers</span></h3>
<p>On Wednesday 10 April 1912 <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s maiden voyage began. Following the embarkation of the crew the passengers began arriving from 9:30 a.m. when the <a href="/wiki/London_and_South_Western_Railway" title="London and South Western Railway">London and South Western Railway</a>'s boat train from <a href="/wiki/London_Waterloo_station" title="London Waterloo station">London Waterloo station</a> reached <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Terminus_railway_station" title="Southampton Terminus railway station">Southampton Terminus railway station</a> on the quayside, right alongside <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s berth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt200961_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt200961-111"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> The large number of Third Class passengers meant that they were the first to board, with First and Second Class passengers following up to within an hour of departure. Stewards showed them to their cabins and First Class passengers were personally greeted by Captain Smith on boarding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010252_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010252-112"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Third Class passengers were inspected for ailments and physical impairments that might lead to them being refused entry to the United States—not a prospect that the White Star Line wished to see, as it would have to carry them back across the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199573_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199573-109"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> 922 passengers were recorded as having embarked <i>Titanic</i> at Southampton. Further passengers were picked up at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cobh" title="Cobh">Queenstown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marriot_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marriot-80"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The maiden voyage began on time at noon. An accident was narrowly averted only a few minutes later as <i>Titanic</i> passed the moored liners <a href="/wiki/SS_City_of_New_York" title="SS City of New York">SS <i>City of New York</i></a> and <i>Oceanic</i>. Her huge displacement caused both of the smaller ships to be lifted by a bulge of water, then dropped into a trough. <i>New York</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s mooring cables could not take the sudden strain and snapped, swinging her around stern-first towards <i>Titanic</i>. A nearby tugboat, <i>Vulcan</i>, came to the rescue by taking <i>New York</i> under tow and Captain Smith ordered <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s engines to be put "full astern".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199576_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199576-113"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> The two ships avoided a collision by a matter of about 4 feet (1.2 m). The incident delayed <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s departure for about an hour while the drifting <i>New York</i> was brought under control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199822_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199822-114"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After making it safely through the complex tides and channels of <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Water" title="Southampton Water">Southampton Water</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Solent" title="Solent">Solent</a>, <i>Titanic</i> headed out into the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>. She headed for the French port of Cherbourg, a journey of 77 nautical miles (89 mi; 143 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201171_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201171-115"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> The weather was windy, very fine but cold and overcast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201179_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201179-116"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Because Cherbourg lacked docking facilities for a ship the size of <i>Titanic</i>, <a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_tender" title="Ship's tender">tenders</a> had to be used to transfer passengers from shore to ship. The White Star Line operated two at Cherbourg, the <a href="/wiki/SS_Traffic_(1911)" title="SS Traffic (1911)">SS <i>Traffic</i></a> and the <a href="/wiki/SS_Nomadic_(1911)" title="SS Nomadic (1911)">SS <i>Nomadic</i></a>. Both had been designed specifically as tenders for the Olympic-class liners and were launched shortly after <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199592_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199592-117"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> (<i>Nomadic</i> is today the only White Star Line ship still afloat.) Four hours after <i>Titanic</i> left Southampton, she arrived at Cherbourg and was met by the tenders. 274 more passengers boarded <i>Titanic</i> and 24 left aboard the tenders to be conveyed to shore. The process was completed within only 90 minutes and at 8 p.m. <i>Titanic</i> <a href="/wiki/Weigh_anchor" title="Weigh anchor">weighed anchor</a> and left for Queenstown<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199593_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas199593-118"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> with the weather continuing cold and windy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201179_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201179-116"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At 11:30 a.m. on Thursday 11 April, <i>Titanic</i> arrived at <a href="/wiki/Cork_Harbour" title="Cork Harbour">Cork Harbour</a> on the south coast of Ireland. It was a partly cloudy but relatively warm day with a brisk wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201179_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201179-116"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Again, the dock facilities were not suitable for a ship of her size, and tenders were used to bring passengers aboard. 113 Third Class and seven Second Class passengers came aboard, while seven passengers left. Among the departures was Father <a href="/wiki/Francis_Browne" title="Francis Browne">Francis Browne</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> trainee, who was a keen photographer and took many photographs aboard <i>Titanic</i>, including the last-ever known photograph of the ship. A decidedly unofficial departure was that of a crew member, stoker John Coffey, a native of Queenstown who sneaked off the ship by hiding under mail bags being transported to shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995100_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995100-119"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i> weighed anchor for the last time at 1:30 p.m. and departed on her westward journey across the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995100_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995100-119"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p><br style="clear:both;"/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_crossing">Atlantic crossing</span></h3>
<p>After leaving Queenstown <i>Titanic</i> followed the Irish coast as far as <a href="/wiki/Fastnet_Rock" title="Fastnet Rock">Fastnet Rock</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201171_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201171-120"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> a distance of some 55 nautical miles (63 mi; 102 km). From there she travelled 1,620 nautical miles (1,860 mi; 3,000 km) along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Circle" title="Great Circle">Great Circle</a> route across the North Atlantic to reach a spot in the ocean known as "the corner" south-east of Newfoundland, where westbound steamers carried out a change of course. <i>Titanic</i> sailed only a few hours past the corner on a <a href="/wiki/Rhumb_line" title="Rhumb line">rhumb line</a> leg of 1,023 nautical miles (1,177 mi; 1,895 km) to <a href="/wiki/Nantucket_Shoals" title="Nantucket Shoals">Nantucket Shoals</a> Light when she made her fatal contact with an iceberg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201175_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201175-121"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> The final leg of the journey would have been 193 nautical miles (222 mi; 357 km) to <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Light" title="Ambrose Light">Ambrose Light</a> and finally to <a href="/wiki/New_York_Harbor" title="New York Harbor">New York Harbor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201173_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201173-122"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first three days of the voyage from Queenstown passed without incident. From 11 April to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_apparent_time" title="Local apparent time">local apparent noon</a> the next day, <i>Titanic</i> covered 484 nautical miles (557 mi; 896 km); the following day, 519 nautical miles (597 mi; 961 km); and by noon on the final day of her voyage, 546 nautical miles (628 mi; 1,011 km). From then until the time of her sinking she travelled another 258 nautical miles (297 mi; 478 km), averaging about 21 knots (24 mph; 39 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201174.E2.80.9375_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201174.E2.80.9375-123"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The weather cleared as she left Ireland under cloudy skies with a headwind. Temperatures remained fairly mild on Saturday 13 April, but the following day <i>Titanic</i> crossed a cold <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">weather front</a> with strong winds and waves of up to 8 feet (2.4 m). These died down as the day progressed until, by the evening of Sunday 14 April, it became clear, calm and very cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201180_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201180-124"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> received a series of warnings from other ships of drifting ice in the area of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks of Newfoundland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan19859_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan19859-125"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Nonetheless the ship continued to steam at full speed, which was standard practice at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912278_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912278-126"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the ship was not trying to set a speed record (a perennial myth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201124_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201124-128"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup>), timekeeping was a priority, and under prevailing maritime practices, ships were often operated at close to full speed, with ice warnings seen as advisories and reliance placed upon lookouts and the watch on the bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912278_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912278-126"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> It was generally believed that ice posed little danger to large vessels. Close calls with ice were not uncommon, and even head-on collisions had not been disastrous. In 1907 <a href="/wiki/SS_Kronprinz_Wilhelm" title="SS Kronprinz Wilhelm">SS <i>Kronprinz Wilhelm</i></a>, a German liner, had rammed an iceberg but still been able to complete her voyage, and Captain Smith himself had declared in 1907 that he could not "imagine any condition which would cause a ship to founder. Modern shipbuilding has gone beyond that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200613_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200613-129"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>i<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sinking">Sinking</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Sinking of the RMS Titanic">Sinking of the RMS <i>Titanic</i></a></div>
<p>At 11:40 p.m. on 14 April (ship's time), lookout <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Fleet" title="Frederick Fleet">Frederick Fleet</a> spotted an iceberg immediately ahead of <i>Titanic</i> and alerted the bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord20052_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord20052-133"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chief_mate" title="Chief mate">First Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/William_McMaster_Murdoch" title="William McMaster Murdoch">William Murdoch</a> ordered the ship to be steered around the obstacle and the engines to be put in reverse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191-134"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> but it was too late; the starboard side of <i>Titanic</i> struck the iceberg, creating a series of holes below the waterline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>j<span>]</span></a></sup> Five of the ship's watertight compartments were breached. It soon became clear that the ship was doomed, as she could not survive more than four compartments being flooded. <i>Titanic</i> began sinking bow-first, with water spilling from compartment to compartment as her angle in the water became steeper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198722_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198722-142"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Those aboard <i>Titanic</i> were ill-prepared for such an emergency. In accordance with accepted practices of the time, where ships were seen as largely unsinkable and lifeboats were intended to transfer passengers to nearby rescue vessels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011116_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011116-143"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>k<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i> only had enough lifeboats to carry about half of those on board; if the ship had carried her full complement of about 3,339 passengers and crew, only about a third could have been accommodated in the lifeboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011109_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011109-146"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> The crew had not been trained adequately in carrying out an evacuation. The officers did not know how many they could safely put aboard the lifeboats and launched many of them barely half-full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621-147"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Third-class passengers were largely left to fend for themselves, causing many of them to become trapped below decks as the ship filled with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284-148"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Women_and_children_first" title="Women and children first">women and children first</a>" protocol was generally followed for the loading of the lifeboats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284-148"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> and most of the male passengers and crew were left aboard.</p>
<p>At 2:20 a.m., two hours and forty minutes after <i>Titanic</i> struck the iceberg, her rate of sinking suddenly increased as her forward deck dipped underwater and the sea poured in through open hatches and grates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011118_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011118-149"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> As her unsupported stern rose out of the water, exposing the propellers, the ship began to break in two between the third and fourth funnels due to the immense strain on the keel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204-150"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> With the bow underwater, and air trapped in the stern, the stern remained afloat and buoyant for a few minutes longer, rising to a nearly vertical angle with hundreds of people still clinging to it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200629_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200629-151"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> before sinking. For many years it was generally believed the ship sank in one piece; however, when <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">the wreck was located</a> many years later, it was discovered that the ship had fully broken in two. All remaining passengers and crew were plunged into lethally cold water with a temperature of 28 °F (−2 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>l<span>]</span></a></sup> Almost all of those in the water died of <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a> or other causes within 15–30 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAldridge200856_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAldridge200856-155"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Only 13 of them were helped into the lifeboats though these had room for almost 500 more people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005103_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005103-156"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Distress signals were sent by wireless, rockets and lamp, but none of the ships that responded was near enough to reach her before she sank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199845.E2.80.9347_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199845.E2.80.9347-157"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> A nearby ship, <i>Californian</i>, which was the last to have been in contact with her before the collision, saw her flares but failed to assist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199864.E2.80.9365_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199864.E2.80.9365-158"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Around 4 a.m., <a href="/wiki/RMS_Carpathia" title="RMS Carpathia">RMS <i>Carpathia</i></a> arrived on the scene in response to <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s earlier distress calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238-159"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> About 710 people survived the disaster and were conveyed by <i>Carpathia</i> to New York, <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s original destination, while 1,500 people lost their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.93111_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.93111-91"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Carpathia</i>'s captain described the place as an ice field that had included 20 large bergs measuring up to 200 feet (61 m) high and numerous smaller bergs, as well as ice floes and debris from <i>Titanic</i>; passengers described being in the middle of a vast white plain of ice, studded with icebergs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242.2C_245_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242.2C_245-160"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p><br style="clear:both;"/></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath_of_sinking">Aftermath of sinking</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_of_Carpathia_in_New_York">Arrival of <i>Carpathia</i> in New York</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg"><img alt="A man wearing a bowler hat and a woman in a shawl embrace among a crowd of people standing in a wooden building" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1760" data-file-width="1409" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg/220px-Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg/330px-Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg/440px-Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The arrival of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s survivors at New York (artist concept)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>m<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/RMS_Carpathia" title="RMS Carpathia"><i>Carpathia</i></a> took three days to reach New York after leaving the scene of the disaster. Her journey was slowed by pack ice, fog, thunderstorms and rough seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011266_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011266-162"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> She was, however, able to pass news to the outside world by wireless about what had happened. The initial reports were confused, leading the American press to report erroneously on 15 April that <i>Titanic</i> was being towed to port by the <a href="/wiki/SS_Drottningholm" title="SS Drottningholm">SS <i>Virginian</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011256_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011256-163"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Later that day, confirmation came through that <i>Titanic</i> had been lost and that most of her passengers and crew had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002169_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002169-164"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> The news attracted crowds of people to the White Star Line's offices in London, New York, Montreal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Southampton,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> Liverpool and Belfast.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It hit hardest in Southampton, whose people suffered the greatest losses from the sinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Southampton2012_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Southampton2012-167"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> 4 out of 5 crew members came from this town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002172_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002172-168"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>n<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Carpathia</i> docked at 9:30 p.m. on 18 April at New York's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pier_54" title="Pier 54">Pier 54</a> and was greeted by some 40,000 people waiting at the quayside in heavy rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002170.2C_172_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002170.2C_172-172"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Immediate relief in the form of clothing and transportation to shelters was provided by the Women's Relief Committee, the <a href="/wiki/Travelers_Aid_Society_of_New_York" title="Travelers Aid Society of New York">Travelers Aid Society of New York</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_Jewish_Women" title="National Council of Jewish Women">Council of Jewish Women</a>, among other organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandau200122.E2.80.9323_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandau200122.E2.80.9323-173"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s surviving passengers did not linger in New York but headed onwards immediately to relatives' homes. Some of the wealthier survivors chartered private trains to take them home, and the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">Pennsylvania Railroad</a> laid on a special train free of charge to take survivors to <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s 214 surviving crew members were taken to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Star_Line" title="Red Star Line">Red Star Line</a>'s steamer <a href="/wiki/SS_Lapland" title="SS Lapland">SS <i>Lapland</i></a>, where they were accommodated in passenger cabins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995183_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995183-174"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Carpathia</i> was hurriedly restocked with food and provisions before resuming her journey to <a href="/wiki/Rijeka" title="Rijeka">Fiume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>. Her crew were given a bonus of a month's wages by Cunard as a reward for their actions, and some of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s passengers joined together to give them an additional bonus of nearly £900 (£77,747 today), divided among the crew members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995184_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995184-175"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The ship's arrival in New York led to a frenzy of press interest, with newspapers competing to be the first to report the survivors' stories. Some reporters bribed their way aboard the <a href="/wiki/Pilot_boat" title="Pilot boat">pilot boat</a> <i>New York</i>, which guided <i>Carpathia</i> into harbour, and one even managed to get onto <i>Carpathia</i> before she docked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995182_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995182-176"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Crowds gathered outside newspaper offices to see the latest reports being posted in the windows or on billboards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995204_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995204-177"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> It took another four days for a complete list of casualties to be compiled and released, adding to the agony of relatives waiting for news of those who had been aboard <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>o<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table align="left" style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">

<p><br style="clear:both;"/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insurance_and_aid_for_survivors">Insurance and aid for survivors</span></h3>
<p>In January 1912, the hulls and equipment of <i>Titanic</i> and <i>Olympic</i> were insured through <a href="/wiki/Lloyd%27s_of_London" title="Lloyd's of London">Lloyd's of London</a>. The total coverage was £1,000,000 (£86,386,100 today) per ship. The policy was to be "free from all average" under £150,000, meaning that the insurers would only pay for damage in excess of that sum. The premium, negotiated by brokers Willis Faber &amp; Company (now <a href="/wiki/Willis_Group" title="Willis Group">Willis Group</a>), was 15 <i>s</i> (75 <i>p</i>) per £100, or £7,500 (£647,896 today) for the term of one year. Lloyd's paid the White Star Line the full sum owed to them within 30 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many charities were set up to help the victims and their families, many of whom lost their sole breadwinner, or, in the case of many Third Class survivors, everything they owned. On 29 April opera stars <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Caruso" title="Enrico Caruso">Enrico Caruso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Garden" title="Mary Garden">Mary Garden</a> and members of the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Opera" title="Metropolitan Opera">Metropolitan Opera</a> raised $12,000 in benefits for victims of the disaster by giving special concerts in which versions of "Autumn" and "Nearer My God To Thee" were part of the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_1912-04-30_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes_1912-04-30-181"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> In Britain, relief funds were organised for the families of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s lost crew members, raising nearly £450,000 (£38,873,745 today). One such fund was still in operation as late as the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998174_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998174-182"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigations_into_the_disaster">Investigations into the disaster</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Wreck_Commissioner%27s_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<p>Even before the survivors arrived in New York, investigations were being planned to discover what had happened, and what could be done to prevent a recurrence. Inquiries were held in both the United States and Great Britain, the former more robustly critical of traditions and practices, and scathing of the failures involved, and the latter broadly more technical and expert-oriented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski201170.E2.80.931_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski201170.E2.80.931-183"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">US Senate's inquiry into the disaster</a> was initiated on 19 April, a day after <i>Carpathia</i> arrived in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199872_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewsterCoulter199872-184"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> The chairman, Senator <a href="/wiki/William_Alden_Smith" title="William Alden Smith">William Alden Smith</a>, wanted to gather accounts from passengers and crew while the events were still fresh in their minds. Smith also needed to subpoena all surviving British passengers and crew while they were still on American soil, which prevented them from returning to the UK before the American inquiry was completed on 25 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senate_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Senate-185"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The British press condemned Smith as an opportunist, insensitively forcing an inquiry as a means of gaining political prestige and seizing "his moment to stand on the world stage". Smith, however, already had a reputation as a campaigner for safety on US railroads, and wanted to investigate any possible malpractices by railroad tycoon <a href="/wiki/J._P._Morgan" title="J. P. Morgan">J. P. Morgan</a>, <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s ultimate owner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998180.E2.80.93186_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998180.E2.80.93186-186"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Wreck_Commissioner%27s_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">British Board of Trade's inquiry into the disaster</a> was headed by <a href="/wiki/John_Bigham,_1st_Viscount_Mersey" title="John Bigham, 1st Viscount Mersey">Lord Mersey</a>, and took place between 2 May and 3 July. Being run by the Board of Trade who had previously approved the ship, it was seen by some as having little interest in its own or White Star's conduct being found negligent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski201170.E2.80.9371.2C_182_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski201170.E2.80.9371.2C_182-187"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Each inquiry took testimony from both passengers and crew of <i>Titanic</i>, crew members of Leyland Line's <i>Californian</i>, Captain <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rostron" title="Arthur Rostron">Arthur Rostron</a> of <i>Carpathia</i> and other experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998192.E2.80.93194_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998192.E2.80.93194-188"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> The British inquiry also took far greater expert testimony, making it the longest and most detailed court of inquiry in British history up to that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998194_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998194-189"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> The two inquiries reached broadly similar conclusions; the regulations on the number of lifeboats that ships had to carry were out of date and inadequate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998195_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998195-190"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain Smith had failed to take proper heed of ice warnings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998189_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998189-191"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> the lifeboats had not been properly filled or crewed, and the collision was the direct result of steaming into a dangerous area at too high a speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998195_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998195-190"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Neither inquiry's findings listed negligence by IMM or the White Star Line as a factor. The US inquiry concluded that since those involved had followed standard practice the disaster was an <a href="/wiki/Act_of_God" title="Act of God">act of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski201167_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski201167-192"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> The British inquiry concluded that Smith had followed long-standing practice not previously been shown to be unsafe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1998189_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1998189-193"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> noting that British ships alone had carried 3.5 million passengers over the previous decade with the loss of just 10 lives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994265_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994265-194"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> and concluded that Smith had done "only that which other skilled men would have done in the same position". The British inquiry also warned that "what was a mistake in the case of the <i>Titanic</i> would without doubt be negligence in any similar case in the future".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1998189_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1998189-193"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The recommendations included strong suggestions for major changes in maritime regulations to implement new safety measures, such as ensuring that more lifeboats were provided, that lifeboat drills were properly carried out and that wireless equipment on passenger ships was manned around the clock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995223_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995223-195"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/International_Ice_Patrol" title="International Ice Patrol">International Ice Patrol</a> was set up to monitor the presence of icebergs in the North Atlantic, and maritime safety regulations were harmonised internationally through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Safety_of_Life_at_Sea" title="International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea">International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea</a>; both measures are still in force today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995310_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995310-196"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_SS_Californian">Role of the SS <i>Californian</i></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Californian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="1000" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Californian.jpg/250px-Californian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Californian.jpg/375px-Californian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Californian.jpg/500px-Californian.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Californian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/SS_Californian" title="SS Californian">SS <i>Californian</i></a>, which had tried to warn <i>Titanic</i> of the danger from pack-ice</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the most controversial issues examined by the inquiries was the role played by <a href="/wiki/SS_Californian" title="SS Californian">SS <i>Californian</i></a>, which had been only a few miles from <i>Titanic</i> but had not picked up her distress calls or responded to her signal rockets. <i>Californian</i> had warned <i>Titanic</i> by radio of the pack ice that was the reason <i>Californian</i> had stopped for the night, but was rebuked by <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s senior wireless operator, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Phillips_(wireless_officer)" title="Jack Phillips (wireless officer)">Jack Phillips</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002160_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002160-197"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Testimony before the British inquiry revealed that at 10:10 p.m., <i>Californian</i> observed the lights of a ship to the south; it was later agreed between Captain <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Lord" title="Stanley Lord">Stanley Lord</a> and Third Officer C.V. Groves (who had relieved Lord of duty at 11:10 p.m.) that this was a passenger liner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002160_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002160-197"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> At 11:50 p.m., the officer had watched that ship's lights flash out, as if she had shut down or turned sharply, and that the port light was now visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002160_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002160-197"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> Morse light signals to the ship, upon Lord's order, were made between 11:30 p.m. and 1:00 a.m., but were not acknowledged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002161_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002161-198"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> If <i>Titanic</i> were as far from the <i>Californian</i> as Lord claimed, then he knew, or should have known, that Morse signals would not be visible. A reasonable and prudent course of action would have been to awaken the wireless operator and to instruct him to attempt to contact <i>Titanic</i> by that method. Had Lord done so, it is possible that he could have reached <i>Titanic</i> in time to save additional lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj1_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsj1-69"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Captain Lord had gone to the chartroom at 11:00 p.m. to spend the night;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002159_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002159-199"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> however, Second Officer Herbert Stone, now on duty, notified Lord at 1:10 a.m. that the ship had fired five rockets. Lord wanted to know if they were company signals, that is, coloured flares used for identification. Stone said that he did not know and that the rockets were all white. Captain Lord instructed the crew to continue to signal the other vessel with the Morse lamp, and went back to sleep. Three more rockets were observed at 1:50 a.m. and Stone noted that the ship looked strange in the water, as if she were listing. At 2:15 a.m., Lord was notified that the ship could no longer be seen. Lord asked again if the lights had had any colours in them, and he was informed that they were all white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004344_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004344-200"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Californian</i> eventually responded. At around 5:30 a.m., Chief Officer George Stewart awakened wireless operator <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Furmstone_Evans" title="Cyril Furmstone Evans">Cyril Furmstone Evans</a>, informed him that rockets had been seen during the night, and asked that he try to communicate with any ship. He got news of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s loss, Captain Lord was notified, and the ship set out to render assistance. She arrived well after <i>Carpathia</i> had already picked up all the survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002164.E2.80.93165_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002164.E2.80.93165-201"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The inquiries found that the ship seen by <i>Californian</i> was in fact <i>Titanic</i> and that it would have been possible for <i>Californian</i> to come to her rescue; therefore, Captain Lord had acted improperly in failing to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler2002191.2C_196_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler2002191.2C_196-202"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>p<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Survivors_and_victims">Survivors and victims</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Titanic_passengers" title="List of Titanic passengers">List of Titanic passengers</a></div>
<p>The number of casualties of the sinking is unclear, due to a number of factors. These include confusion over the passenger list, which included some names of people who cancelled their trip at the last minute, and the fact that several passengers travelled under aliases for various reasons and were therefore double-counted on the casualty lists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998239_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998239-205"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> The death toll has been put at between 1,490 and 1,635 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1976197_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1976197-206"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> The tables below use figures from the British <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> report on the disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.93111_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.93111-91"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders sortable" style="margin: 1em auto; text-align: right;">
<tr>
<th scope="col">Age/<wbr></wbr>gender</th>
<th scope="col">Class/<wbr></wbr>crew</th>
