<p>Fewer than a third of those aboard <i>Titanic</i> survived the disaster. Some survivors died shortly afterwards; injuries and the effects of exposure caused the deaths of several of those brought aboard <i>Carpathia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994179_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994179-207"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> The figures show stark differences in the survival rates of the different classes aboard <i>Titanic</i>. Although only 3 percent of first-class women were lost, 54 percent of those in third class died. Similarly, five of six first-class and all second-class children survived, but 52 of the 79 in third class perished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells199994_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells199994-208"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The last living survivor, <a href="/wiki/Millvina_Dean" title="Millvina Dean">Millvina Dean</a> from England, who at only nine weeks old was the youngest passenger on board, died aged 97 on 31 May 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> A special survivor was crew member <a href="/wiki/Violet_Jessop" title="Violet Jessop">Violet Jessop</a> who survived the sinkings of both <i>Titanic</i> and <i>Britannic</i> and was aboard <i>Olympic</i> when she was rammed in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jessop_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jessop-210"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retrieval_and_burial_of_the_dead">Retrieval and burial of the dead</span></h3>
<p>Once the massive loss of life became known, White Star Line chartered the cable ship <a href="/wiki/CS_Mackay-Bennett" title="CS Mackay-Bennett">CS <i>Mackay-Bennett</i></a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_Halifax" title="City of Halifax">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a>, to retrieve bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995228_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995228-211"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> Three other Canadian ships followed in the search: the cable ship <i>Minia</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995232_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995232-212"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> lighthouse supply ship <i>Montmagny</i> and <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">sealing vessel</a> <i>Algerine</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995234_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995234-213"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Each ship left with embalming supplies, undertakers, and clergy. Of the 333 victims that were eventually recovered, 328 were retrieved by the Canadian ships and five more by passing North Atlantic steamships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995225_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995225-214"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>q<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first ship to reach the site of the sinking, the CS <i>Mackay-Bennett</i> found so many bodies that the embalming supplies aboard were quickly exhausted. Health regulations required that only embalmed bodies could be returned to port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov_ns_ca_victims_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-gov_ns_ca_victims-217"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain Larnder of the <i>Mackay-Bennett</i> and undertakers aboard decided to preserve only the bodies of first class passengers, justifying their decision by the need to visually identify wealthy men to resolve any disputes over large estates. As a result, many third class passengers and crew were buried at sea. Larnder identified many of those buried at sea as crew members by their clothing, and stated that as a mariner, he himself would be contented to be buried at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-funeral_ship_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-funeral_ship-218"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Bodies recovered were preserved for transport to Halifax, the closest city to the sinking with direct rail and steamship connections. The Halifax coroner, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_Henry_Barnstead&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John Henry Barnstead (page does not exist)">John Henry Barnstead</a>, developed a detailed system to identify bodies and safeguard personal possessions. Relatives from across North America came to identify and claim bodies. A large temporary morgue was set up in the <a href="/wiki/Curling" title="Curling">curling</a> rink of the <a href="/wiki/Mayflower_Curling_Club" title="Mayflower Curling Club">Mayflower Curling Club</a> and undertakers were called in from all across eastern Canada to assist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-funeral_ship_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-funeral_ship-218"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Some bodies were shipped to be buried in their home towns across North America and Europe. About two-thirds of the bodies were identified. Unidentified victims were buried with simple numbers based on the order in which their bodies were discovered. The majority of recovered victims, 150 bodies, were buried in three Halifax cemeteries, the largest being <a href="/wiki/Fairview_Cemetery,_Halifax,_Nova_Scotia" title="Fairview Cemetery, Halifax, Nova Scotia">Fairview Lawn Cemetery</a> followed by the nearby <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olivet_Cemetery_(Halifax)" title="Mount Olivet Cemetery (Halifax)">Mount Olivet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baron_de_Hirsch_Cemetery,_Halifax" title="Baron de Hirsch Cemetery, Halifax">Baron de Hirsch</a> cemeteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995244.E2.80.93245_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995244.E2.80.93245-219"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-May 1912, <a href="/wiki/RMS_Oceanic_(1899)" title="RMS Oceanic (1899)">RMS <i>Oceanic</i></a> recovered three bodies over 200 miles (320 km) from the site of the sinking who were among the original occupants of Collapsible A. When Fifth Officer <a href="/wiki/Harold_Lowe" title="Harold Lowe">Harold Lowe</a> and six crewmen returned to the wreck site sometime after the sinking in a lifeboat to pick up survivors, they rescued a dozen males and one female from Collapsible A, but left the dead bodies of three of its occupants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>r<span>]</span></a></sup> After their retrieval from Collapsible A by <i>Oceanic</i>, the bodies were buried at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242.E2.80.93243_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242.E2.80.93243-221"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The last <i>Titanic</i> body recovered was steward James McGrady, Body No. 330, found by the chartered Newfoundland sealing vessel <i>Algerine</i> on May 22 and buried at <a href="/wiki/Fairview_Cemetery,_Halifax,_Nova_Scotia" title="Fairview Cemetery, Halifax, Nova Scotia">Fairview Lawn Cemetery</a> in Halifax on June 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Only 333 bodies of <i>Titanic</i> victims were recovered, one in five of the over 1500 victims. Some bodies sank with the ship while currents quickly dispersed bodies and wreckage across hundreds of miles making them difficult to recover. By June one of the last search ships reported that life jackets supporting bodies were coming apart and releasing bodies to sink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wreck">Wreck</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">Wreck of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="700" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/250px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/375px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/500px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The bow of the wrecked RMS <i>Titanic</i>, photographed in June 2004</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was long thought to have sunk in one piece and, over the years, many schemes were put forward for raising the wreck. None came to fruition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012166_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012166-224"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> The fundamental problem was the sheer difficulty of finding and reaching a wreck that lies over 12,000 feet (3,700 m) below the surface, in a location where the water pressure is over 6,500 pounds per square inch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012221_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012221-225"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> A number of expeditions were mounted to find <i>Titanic</i> but it was not until 1 September 1985 that a Franco-American expedition succeeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012171.E2.80.93172_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012171.E2.80.93172-226"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The team discovered that <i>Titanic</i> had in fact split apart, probably near or at the surface, before sinking to the seabed. The separated bow and stern sections lie about a third of a mile (0.6 km) apart in a canyon on the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> off the coast of Newfoundland. They are located 13.2 miles (21.2 km) from the inaccurate coordinates given by <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s radio operators on the night of her sinking,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126.E2.80.93127_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126.E2.80.93127-228"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> and approximately 715 miles (1,151 km) from Halifax and 1,250 miles (2,012 km) from New York.</p>
<p>Both sections hit the sea bed at considerable speed, causing the bow to crumple and the stern to collapse entirely. The bow is by far the more intact section and still contains some surprisingly intact interiors. In contrast, the stern is completely wrecked; its decks have pancaked down on top of each other and much of the hull plating was torn off and lies scattered across the sea floor. The much greater level of damage to the stern is probably due to structural damage incurred during the sinking. Thus weakened, the remainder of the stern was flattened by the impact with the sea bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987205_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987205-229"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The two sections are surrounded by a debris field measuring approximately 5 by 3 miles (8.0 km × 4.8 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanfield8_March_2012_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanfield8_March_2012-230"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> It contains hundreds of thousands of items, such as pieces of the ship, furniture, dinnerware and personal items, which fell from the ship as she sank or were ejected when the bow and stern impacted on the sea floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987203_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987203-231"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> The debris field was also the last resting place of a number of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s victims. Most of the bodies and clothes were consumed by sea creatures and bacteria, leaving pairs of shoes and boots—which have proved to be inedible—as the only sign that bodies once lay there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987207_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987207-232"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Since its discovery, the wreck of <i>Titanic</i> has been revisited numerous times by explorers, scientists, filmmakers, tourists and salvagers, who have recovered thousands of items from the debris field for conservation and public display. The ship's condition has deteriorated significantly in recent years, partly due to accidental damage caused by submersibles but mainly because of an accelerating rate of growth of iron-eating bacteria on the hull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012171_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012171-233"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been estimated that within the next 50 years the hull and structure of <i>Titanic</i> will collapse entirely, eventually leaving only the more durable interior fittings of the ship intermingled with a pile of rust on the sea floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrosbieMortimer2006last_page_.28no_page_number_specified.29_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrosbieMortimer2006last_page_.28no_page_number_specified.29-234"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many artefacts from <i>Titanic</i> have been recovered from the sea bed by RMS Titanic Inc., which exhibits them in touring exhibitions around the world and in a permanent exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Luxor_Las_Vegas" title="Luxor Las Vegas">Luxor Las Vegas</a> hotel and casino in <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Valley" title="Las Vegas Valley">Las Vegas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012259_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012259-235"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> A number of other museums exhibit artefacts either donated by survivors or retrieved from the floating bodies of victims of the disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012248.2C_251_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012248.2C_251-236"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 16 April 2012, a day after the 100th anniversary of the sinking, photos were released showing possible human remains resting on the ocean floor. The photos, taken by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a> during an expedition led by <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">NOAA</a> in 2004, show a boot and a coat close to <i>Titanic'</i>s stern which experts called "compelling evidence" that it's the spot where somebody came to rest, and that human remains could be buried in the sediment beneath them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> The wreck of the Titanic falls under the scope of the 2001 <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_Convention_on_the_Protection_of_the_Underwater_Cultural_Heritage" title="UNESCO Convention on the Protection of the Underwater Cultural Heritage">UNESCO Convention on the Protection of the Underwater Cultural Heritage</a>. This means that all States party to the convention will prohibit the pillaging, commercial exploitation, sale and dispersion of the wreck and its artefacts. Because of the location of the wreck in <a href="/wiki/International_waters" title="International waters">international waters</a> and the lack of any exclusive jurisdiction over the wreckage area, the convention provides a state co-operation system, by which States inform each other of any potential activity concerning ancient shipwreck sites, like the Titanic, and co-operate to prevent unscientific or unethical interventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1740" data-file-width="2570" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg/250px-USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg/375px-USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg/500px-USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USCG_International_Ice_Patrol_C-130.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An ice patrol aircraft inspecting an iceberg</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the disaster, recommendations were made by both the British and American Boards of Inquiry stating that ships should carry enough lifeboats for all aboard, mandated lifeboat drills would be implemented, lifeboat inspections would be conducted, etc. Many of these recommendations were incorporated into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Safety_of_Life_at_Sea" title="International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea">International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea</a> passed in 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> The convention has been updated by periodic amendments, with a completely new version adopted in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMO-1974_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMO-1974-242"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Signatories to the convention followed up with national legislation to implement the new standards. For example in Britain, new “Rules for Life Saving Appliances” were passed by the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> on May 8, 1914 and then applied at a meeting of British steamship companies in Liverpool in June 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Further, the United States government passed the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Act_of_1912" title="Radio Act of 1912">Radio Act of 1912</a>. This act, along with the International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea, stated that radio communications on passenger ships would be operated 24 hours a day, along with a secondary power supply, so as not to miss distress calls. Also, the Radio Act of 1912 required ships to maintain contact with vessels in their vicinity as well as coastal onshore radio stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marconi_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-marconi-244"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, it was agreed in the International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea that the firing of red rockets from a ship must be interpreted as a sign of need for help. Once the Radio Act of 1912 was passed it was agreed that rockets at sea would be interpreted as distress signals only, thus removing any possible misinterpretation from other ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marconi_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-marconi-244"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Finally, the disaster led to the formation and international funding of the <a href="/wiki/International_Ice_Patrol" title="International Ice Patrol">International Ice Patrol</a>, an agency of the United States Coast Guard that to the present day monitors and reports on the location of North Atlantic Ocean icebergs that could pose a threat to transatlantic sea traffic. Coast Guard aircraft conduct the primary reconnaissance. In addition, information is collected from ships operating in or passing through the ice area. Except for the years of the two World Wars, the International Ice Patrol has worked each season since 1913. During the period there has not been a single reported loss of life or property due to collision with an iceberg in the patrol area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural">Cultural</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_legacy_of_RMS_Titanic" title="Cultural legacy of RMS Titanic">Cultural legacy of RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1311" data-file-width="1735" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg/250px-Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg/375px-Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg/500px-Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_Belfast_HDR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Titanic_Belfast" title="Titanic Belfast">Titanic Belfast</a>, 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> has gone down in history as the ship that was called unsinkable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246"><span>[</span>s<span>]</span></a></sup> For more than 100 years she has been the inspiration of fiction and non-fiction. She is commemorated by monuments for the dead and by museums exhibiting artefacts from the wreck. Just after the sinking memorial postcards sold in huge numbers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995327_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995327-247"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> together with memorabilia ranging from tin candy boxes to plates, whiskey jiggers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995329.E2.80.93330_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1995329.E2.80.93330-248"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> and even black mourning teddy bears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManiera200350_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManiera200350-249"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> Several survivors wrote books about their experiences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasor200177_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasor200177-250"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> but it was not until 1955 the first historically accurate book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Night_to_Remember_(book)" title="A Night to Remember (book)">A Night to Remember</a></i> was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005xii_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005xii-251"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first film about the disaster, <i><a href="/wiki/Saved_from_the_Titanic" title="Saved from the Titanic">Saved from the Titanic</a></i>, was released only 29 days after the ship sank and had an actual survivor as its star—the silent film actress <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Gibson" title="Dorothy Gibson">Dorothy Gibson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012267_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012267-252"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup> The British film <a href="/wiki/A_Night_to_Remember_(1958_film)" title="A Night to Remember (1958 film)"><i>A Night to Remember</i> (1958)</a> is still widely regarded as the most historically accurate movie portrayal of the sinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeyer2012104_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeyer2012104-253"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> The most financially successful by far has been <a href="/wiki/James_Cameron" title="James Cameron">James Cameron</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">Titanic</a></i> (1997), which became the highest-grossing film in history up to that time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParisi1998223_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParisi1998223-254"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as the winner of 11 <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Oscars</a> at the <a href="/wiki/70th_Academy_Awards" title="70th Academy Awards">70th Academy Awards</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Best Picture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Director" title="Academy Award for Best Director">Best Director</a> for Cameron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Titanic</i> disaster was commemorated through a variety of memorials and monuments to the victims, erected in several English-speaking countries and in particular in cities that had suffered notable losses. These included Southampton, Liverpool and Belfast in the United Kingdom; New York and Washington, D.C. in the United States; and Cobh (formerly Queenstown) in Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012262.E2.80.93263_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012262.E2.80.93263-256"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> A number of museums around the world have displays on <i>Titanic</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, the ship is commemorated by the <a href="/wiki/Titanic_Belfast" title="Titanic Belfast">Titanic Belfast</a> visitor attraction, opened on 31 March 2012, that stands on the site of the shipyard where <i>Titanic</i> was built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC_News31_March_2012_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC_News31_March_2012-257"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>RMS Titanic Inc., which is authorised to salvage the wreck site, has a permanent <i>Titanic</i> exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Luxor_Las_Vegas" title="Luxor Las Vegas">Luxor Las Vegas</a> hotel and casino in <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> which features a 22-ton slab of the ship's hull. It also runs an exhibition which travels around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012252_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012252-258"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> In Nova Scotia, Halifax's <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Museum_of_the_Atlantic" title="Maritime Museum of the Atlantic">Maritime Museum of the Atlantic</a> displays items that were recovered from the sea a few days after the disaster. They include pieces of woodwork such as panelling from the ship's First Class Lounge and an original deckchair,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012251_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012251-259"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as objects removed from the victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012261_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012261-260"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2012 the centenary was marked by plays, radio programmes, parades, exhibition and special trips to the site of the sinking together with commemorative stamps and coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Southampton2012_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Southampton2012-167"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITV_com_Titanic_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITV_com_Titanic-261"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2009-04-15_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2009-04-15-263"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In a frequently commented on literary coincidence, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Robertson" title="Morgan Robertson">Morgan Robertson</a> authored a novel called <i><a href="/wiki/Futility,_or_the_Wreck_of_the_Titan" title="Futility, or the Wreck of the Titan">Futility</a></i> in 1898 about a fictional British passenger liner with the plot bearing a number of similarities to the Titanic disaster. In the novel the ship is the SS Titan, a four stacked liner, the largest in the world and considered unsinkable. But like the Titanic she sinks on her maiden voyage after hitting an iceberg and does not have enough lifeboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Titanic_-_Futilityu_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Titanic_-_Futilityu-265"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

Names of decks are listed to the right (Starting at top on Boat deck, going from A through F and ending on Lower deck at the waterline). Areas of damage made by the iceberg are shown in green. The scale's smallest unit is 10 feet (3.0 m) and its total length is 400 feet (120 m).</center>

<div class="NavHead" style="background:transparent;text-align:left; text-align: center;">Timeline of RMS <i>Titanic</i> </div>
<div class="NavContent" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;">
<ul>
<li>17 September 1908: Ship ordered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beveridge_09Chap1_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beveridge_09Chap1-266"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>31 May 1911: Ship launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1 April 1912: Trials completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005148_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005148-268"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>10 April, noon: Maiden voyage starts. Leaves Southampton dock, narrowly escaping collision with American liner <i>New York</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005148_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005148-268"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>10 April, 19:00: Stops at Cherbourg for passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005148_268-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005148-268"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>10 April, 21:00: Leaves Cherbourg for Queenstown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005148_268-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005148-268"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>11 April, 12:30: Stops at Queenstown for passengers and mail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005148_268-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005148-268"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>11 April, 14:00: Leaves Queenstown for New York<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005148_268-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005148-268"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>14 April, 23:40: Collision with iceberg (Latitude 41° 46′ N, Longitude 50° 14′ W).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005149_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005149-269"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiptime_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-shiptime-270"><span>[</span>t<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>15 April, 00:45: First boat, No. 7, lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005150_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005150-271"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiptime_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-shiptime-270"><span>[</span>t<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>15 April, 02:05: Last boat, Collapsible D, lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005150_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005150-271"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiptime_270-2"><a href="#cite_note-shiptime-270"><span>[</span>t<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>15 April, 02:20: Foundering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005150_271-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005150-271"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiptime_270-3"><a href="#cite_note-shiptime-270"><span>[</span>t<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>15 April, 03:30–08:50: Rescue of survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005150_271-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005150-271"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiptime_270-4"><a href="#cite_note-shiptime-270"><span>[</span>t<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>19 April – 25 May: US inquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senate_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Senate-185"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>2 May – 3 July: British inquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1 September 1985: Discovery of wreck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012171.E2.80.93172_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012171.E2.80.93172-226"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Replica_Titanic">Replica Titanic</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Replica_Titanic" title="Replica Titanic">Replica Titanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titanic_II" title="Titanic II">Titanic II</a></div>
<p>There have been several proposals and studies for a project to build a <a href="/wiki/Ship_replica" title="Ship replica">replica ship</a> based on the Titanic . A project by South African businessman Sarel Gaus was abandoned in 2006, and a project by Australian businessman Clive Palmer was announced in 2012, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Titanic_II" title="Titanic II">Titanic II</a></i>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carlisle would leave the project in 1910, before the ships were launched, when he became a shareholder in <a href="/wiki/Axel_Welin" title="Axel Welin">Welin Davit &amp; Engineering Company Ltd</a>, the firm making the ship's davits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIPCarlisle_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIPCarlisle-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Wilding was sacked following the <i>Titanic</i> disaster, having apparently been blamed by Pirrie, unfairly, for the ship's loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199820_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCluskie199820-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was kept off-limits to passengers; the famous "flying" scene at the ship's bow from the 1997 film <i>Titanic</i> would not have been permitted in real life.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This photo is probably of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s sister ship, <i>Olympic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge2008100_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge2008100-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Measurement of lifeboats: <b>1–2:</b> 25'2" long by 7'2" wide by 3'2" deep; 326.6 cubic feet (9.25 m<sup>3</sup>); <b>3–16:</b> 30' long by 9'1" wide by 4' deep; 655.2 cubic feet (18.55 m<sup>3</sup>) and <b>A–D:</b> 27'5" long by 8' wide by 3' deep; 376.6 cubic feet (10.66 m<sup>3</sup>)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since 1894, when the largest passenger ship under consideration was the <a href="/wiki/Cunard_Line" title="Cunard Line">Cunard Line</a>'s 13,000-ton <a href="/wiki/RMS_Lucania" title="RMS Lucania"><i>Lucania</i></a>, the Board of Trade had made no provision to increase the existing scale regarding the number of required lifeboats for larger ships, such as the 46,000-ton <i>Titanic</i>. Sir Alfred Chalmers, nautical adviser to the Board of Trade from 1896 to 1911, had considered the matter of adjusting the scale "from time to time", but because he not only assumed that experienced sailors would need to be carried "uselessly" aboard ship only to lower and man the extra lifeboats, but also anticipated the difficulty in getting away a greater number than 16 boats in any emergency, he "did not consider it necessary to increase [the scale]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritishInq_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritishInq-67"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He expressed deep disappointment about the decision before the voyage, but was presumably greatly relieved afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010241_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010241-95"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Titanic</i> also had a ship's cat, Jenny, who gave birth to a litter of kittens shortly before the ship's maiden voyage; all perished in the sinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010246_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010246-100"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Known afterward as the "Unsinkable Molly Brown" due to her efforts in helping other passengers while the ship sank</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Captain Edward Smith had been in command of <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s sister <i>Olympic</i> when she in 1911 collided with a warship. Even though that ship was designed to sink others by ramming them, it suffered greater damage than <i>Olympic</i>, thereby strengthening the image of the class being unsinkable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-titanico_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-titanico-130"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The official enquiry found that damage extended about 300 feet, but both Edward Wilding's testimony and modern <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> surveys of the <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">wreck</a> suggest the total area was perhaps a few narrow openings totalling perhaps no more than 12 to 13 square feet (1.1 to 1.2 m<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReport_on_the_Loss_of_the_.22Titanic.22._.28s.s..29question_20422.2C_Day_19_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReport_on_the_Loss_of_the_.22Titanic.22._.28s.s..29question_20422.2C_Day_19-135"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad1997_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad1997-136"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Considerable emphasis has also been placed on the quality and strength of the <a href="/wiki/Rivet" title="Rivet">rivets</a> securing Titanic's hull sections together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad1997_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad1997-136"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Materials_Today.27.27.2C_2008_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Materials_Today.27.27.2C_2008-137"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCartyFoecke2012.5B.5BCategory:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2012.5D.5D.3Csup_class.3D.22noprint_Inline-Template_.22_style.3D.22white-space:nowrap.3B.22.3E.5B.3Ci.3E.5B.5BWikipedia:Citing_sources.7C.3Cspan_title.3D.22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears..7FUNIQf4e015653512d590-nowiki-00000232-QINU.7F_.28March_2012.29.22.3Epage.C2.A0needed.3C.2Fspan.3E.5D.5D.3C.2Fi.3E.5D.3C.2Fsup.3E_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCartyFoecke2012.5B.5BCategory:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2012.5D.5D.3Csup_class.3D.22noprint_Inline-Template_.22_style.3D.22white-space:nowrap.3B.22.3E.5B.3Ci.3E.5B.5BWikipedia:Citing_sources.7C.3Cspan_title.3D.22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears..7FUNIQf4e015653512d590-nowiki-00000232-QINU.7F_.28March_2012.29.22.3Epage.C2.A0needed.3C.2Fspan.3E.5D.5D.3C.2Fi.3E.5D.3C.2Fsup.3E-138"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad2008_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad2008-139"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerhoeven200749_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerhoeven200749-140"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An incident confirmed this philosophy while <i>Titanic</i> was under construction: the White Star liner <i>Republic</i> was involved in a collision and sank. Even though she did not have enough lifeboats for all passengers, they were all saved because the ship was able to stay afloat long enough for them to be ferried to ships coming to assist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside200429_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside200429-144"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Life expectancy in such temperatures is often under 15 minutes even for people who are young and fit. The victims would have died from <a href="/wiki/Involuntary_action" title="Involuntary action">bodily reactions</a> to freezing water rather than <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a> (loss of core temperature). Plunged into freezing seas, around 20% of victims die within 2 minutes from <a href="/wiki/Cold_shock_response" title="Cold shock response">cold shock</a> (uncontrolled <a href="/wiki/Hyperventilation" title="Hyperventilation">rapid breathing</a> and gasping causing water inhalation, massive increase in blood pressure and cardiac strain leading to <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Panic" title="Panic">panic</a>), another 50% die within 15–30 minutes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_incapacitation" title="Cold incapacitation">cold incapacitation</a> (inability to use or control limbs and hands for swimming or gripping, as the body 'protectively' shuts down peripheral muscles to protect its core),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> and exhaustion and unconsciousness cause <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drowning</a>, claiming the rest within a similar time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to an eyewitness report, there "were many pathetic scenes" when <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s survivors disembarked at New York<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-171"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-171">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Salvation Army newspaper, <i>The War Cry</i>, reported that "none but a heart of stone would be unmoved in the presence of such anguish. Night and day that crowd of pale, anxious faces had been waiting patiently for the news that did not come. Nearly every one in the crowd had lost a relative."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011261_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011261-169"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> It was not until 17 April that the first incomplete lists of survivors came through, delayed by poor communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011262_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011262-170"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On 23 April, the <i>Daily Mail</i> reported: "Late in the afternoon hope died out. The waiting crowds thinned, and silent men and women sought their homes. In the humbler homes of Southampton there is scarcely a family who has not lost a relative or friend. Children returning from school appreciated something of tragedy, and woeful little faces were turned to the darkened, fatherless homes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998173_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998173-178"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lord protested his innocence to the end of his life, and many researchers have asserted that the known positions of <i>Titanic</i> and <i>Californian</i> make it impossible that the former was the infamous "mystery ship", a topic which has "generated ... millions of words and ... hours of heated debates" and continues to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most of the bodies were numbered, however, the five passengers <a href="/wiki/Burial_at_sea" title="Burial at sea">buried at sea</a> by <i>Carpathia</i> went unnumbered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov_ns_ca_bodies_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-gov_ns_ca_bodies-215"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Beattie, a first class passenger, and two crew members, a fireman and a seaman.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-246"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-246">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An example is Daniel Butler's book about RMS <i>Titanic</i>, titled <i>Unsinkable</i></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-shiptime-270"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-shiptime_270-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-shiptime_270-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-shiptime_270-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-shiptime_270-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-shiptime_270-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Ship's time; at the time of the collision, <i>Titanic</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s clocks were set to 2 hours 2 minutes ahead of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">Eastern Time Zone</a> and 2 hours 58 minutes behind <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">Greenwich Mean Time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201178_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201178-272"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEGill2010121-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010121_30-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGill2010121_30-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGill2010">Gill 2010</a>, p. 121.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201179-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201179_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011">Hutchings &amp; de Kerbrech 2011</a>, p. 79.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Despite later myth, featured for example in the <a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">1997 film <i>Titanic</i></a>, the ship <i>Titanic</i> was not attempting to set a transatlantic speed record; the White Star Line had made a conscious decision not to compete with their rivals <a href="/wiki/Cunard_Line" title="Cunard Line">Cunard</a> on speed, but instead to focus on size and luxury.</span></li>

<p><b>Investigations:</b></p>
